44:2289:98219:163:14:72:540:322:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Figure 5:  The left panel shows the distribution of aggregate output for the benchmark
44:2290:98305:14:15:72:540:308:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model  and  loglinear  model  at  an  annual  frequency.   The  right  panel  shows  these  for
44:2291:98400:15:14:72:540:293:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:shocks at a quadrennial frequency.   The difference between the two frequencies is that
44:2292:98487:14:15:72:540:279:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:shocks are larger (their volatility is higher) at a quadrennial than at an annual frequency.
44:2293:98579:15:14:72:539:264:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:The benchmark model has (σ,θ,ε)=(0.9,0.5,0.001) and no labor reallocation.  Note that
44:2294:98664:14:33:72:298:250:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the scales are different in these two figures.
44:2295:98710:33:18:89:539:217:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:We  also  consider  the  response  of  aggregate  output  to  shocks  to  specific  industries,
44:2296:98805:18:18:72:540:199:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:using our benchmark calibration. It turns out that for a large negative shock, the “oil and
44:2297:98896:18:18:72:540:181:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:gas” industry produces the largest negative response in aggregate output, despite the fact
44:2298:98986:18:18:72:540:163:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:it is not the largest industry in the economy.   Figure 6 plots the response of aggregate
44:2299:99075:18:17:72:540:145:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:output for shocks to the oil and gas industry as well as for the “retail trade (excluding
44:2300:99164:17:18:72:540:128:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:automobiles)” industry. The retail trade industry has a similar sales share, and therefore,
44:2301:99255:18:18:72:540:110:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:to a first-order, both industries are equally important.  As expected, the nonlinear model
44:2302:99345:18:18:72:540:92:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:amplifies negative shocks and mitigates positive shocks.   However,  whereas output is
44:2303:99431:18:0:72:540:74:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:roughly loglinear for  shocks to retail trade,  output is highly  nonlinear  with respect to
45:2304:99525:0:18:72:184:708:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:shocks to oil and gas.
45:2305:99547:18:18:89:539:690:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:The intuition for this asymmetry comes from the examples in Figures 3 and 4. Oil and
45:2306:99631:18:18:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:gas are approximately universal inputs, so that the downstream elasticity of substitution
45:2307:99720:18:18:72:539:654:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(in consumption)σis less relevant and the upstream elasticity of substitution (in produc-
45:2308:99809:18:18:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:tion)θis more relevant.  Sinceθ-σin our calibration, this means that output is more
45:2309:99892:18:18:72:540:618:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:nonlinear in shocks to oil and gas than in shocks to retail trade. Furthermore, since oil and
45:2310:99985:18:18:72:540:600:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:gas have a relatively low share of intermediate input usage, this means that, in response
45:2311:100074:18:18:72:540:582:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:to a negative shock, resources cannot be reallocated to boost the production of oil and
45:2312:100161:18:18:72:540:564:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:gas.  This means that we are closer to the economy depicted in Figure 4 than the one in
45:2313:100248:18:18:72:540:546:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Figure 3, with a correspondingly lower GE elasticity of substitution between oil and gas
45:2314:100336:18:18:72:338:528:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:and everything else due to the lack of reallocation.
45:2315:100388:18:18:89:539:510:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:The strong asymmetry between the effects of positive and negative shocks is consistent
45:2316:100474:18:18:72:540:492:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:with  the  empirical  findings  of  Hamilton  (2003)  that  oil  price  increases  are  much  more
45:2317:100572:18:36:72:262:474:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f5:B:important than oil price decreases.41
45:2318:100609:36:26:72:242:438:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:Results with Adjustment Costs
45:2319:100638:26:18:72:540:412:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:For the model with adjustment costs, we choose the value of the adjustment cost parameter
45:2320:100727:18:18:72:540:394:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:B:κso  that,  given  the  microecononomic  elasticities  of  substitution,  the  model  matches
45:2321:100820:18:18:72:540:376:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the  volatility  of  the  Domar  weights  at  an  annual  frequency  (when  the  model  already
45:2322:100915:18:18:72:540:358:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:overshoots without adjustment costs, we set them to zero). We then keep the same value
45:2323:101001:18:18:72:540:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:ofκwhen we move quadrennial frequency.  By picking a suitable value forκ, even the
45:2324:101083:18:18:72:540:322:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model with fully mobile labor can match the volatility of the Domar weights.  We report
45:2325:101170:18:17:72:539:304:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:these results in Table 2.   Interestingly, once we pickκto match the volatility of Domar
45:2326:101258:17:18:72:540:287:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:weights at annual frequency, the model also roughly matches the volatility of the Domar
45:2327:101345:18:18:72:540:269:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:weights at a quadrennial frequency.   The results are consistent with what we found in
45:2328:101431:18:18:72:540:251:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Table 1.  In the final column of Table 2 we also report the value of resources destroyed by

46:2367:103410:40:26:71:263:224:14:g_d0_f4:14:g_d0_f4:C:6.2    The Effect of Oil Shocks
46:2368:103441:26:18:71:539:198:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:In this section, we use the oil shocks of the 1970s to demonstrate the way nonlinearities
46:2369:103530:18:19:71:539:180:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:can amplify the macroeconomic impact of industry-level shocks.42To recap the history,
46:2370:103615:19:16:82:539:161:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:42Although our structural model suggests that the “oil and natural gas” extraction industry is important,
46:2371:103720:16:12:72:540:145:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:it abstracts away from trade, by assuming all intermediate inputs are sourced domestically, with net imports
46:2372:103828:12:12:72:540:133:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:showing up only in final demand.  Hence, the Domar weight of the oil and natural gas industry measures
46:2373:103930:12:12:72:539:121:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:domestic production, rather than domestic consumption.  Since the oil price shocks did not directly affect
46:2374:104036:12:12:72:540:109:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:the productivity of domestic oil production, this means that they are not measured in our sectoral TFP data
46:2375:104143:12:12:72:540:97:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:(which is for domestic production).  Furthermore, our industry classification is too coarse to isolate crude
46:2376:104251:12:0:72:484:85:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:oil separately from other petrochemicals. For this reason, we use global (rather than US) data.

47:2388:104939:28:14:72:540:261:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Table 2:  Simulated and estimated moments for the model with adjustment costs.   The
47:2389:105023:14:15:72:539:247:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:simulated  moments  are  calculated  from  10,000  draws.   The  parametertmeasures  the
47:2390:105111:15:14:71:539:232:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f6:B:length of the time interval for the shocks: annual and quadrennial. Finally, the column∆
47:2391:105199:14:0:71:221:218:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:is the share of lost resources.
48:2392:105232:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:starting in 1973,  coordinated action by OPEC caused the price of crude oil to increase
48:2393:105319:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:from  $3.5  per  barrel  in  1972  to  $11  per  barrel  in  1974.   In  1979,  OPEC  implemented  a
48:2394:105419:18:18:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:second round of quantity restrictions which caused the price of crude to soar to $31 per
48:2395:105507:18:18:72:540:654:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:barrel.  Shortly after this, the Iranian revolution of 1979 and the ensuing Iraqi invasion
48:2396:105597:18:18:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:of Iran caused further disruptions to global crude oil supply.  The price peaked at $37 in
48:2397:105687:18:18:72:540:618:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:1980. Starting in the early 1980s, with the abdication of the Shah, OPEC’s pricing structure
48:2398:105779:18:18:72:540:600:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:collapsed and, in a bid to maintain its market share, Saudi Arabia flooded the market with
48:2399:105869:18:18:72:540:582:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:inexpensive oil.  In real terms, the price of crude oil declined back to its pre-crisis levels
48:2400:105963:18:18:72:115:564:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:by 1986.
48:2401:105971:18:18:89:539:546:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:We adopt a non-parametric approach which allows us to account for the second-order
48:2402:106053:18:18:72:540:528:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:macroeconomic impact of microeconomic shocks using ex-post data.   Instead of trying
48:2403:106137:18:18:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:to predict how Domar weights change in response to a shock as would be required for
48:2404:106220:18:18:72:540:492:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:a counterfactual exercise, we can simply observe it.  Formally, we rely on the following
48:2405:106308:18:27:72:105:474:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:result.
48:2406:106315:27:36:72:385:447:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f7:C:Proposition 10.Up to the second order in the vector∆, we have
48:2407:106376:36:0:136:251:411:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f6:A:log(Y(A+ ∆)/Y(A))=
48:2408:106394:0:16:255:261:419:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:1
48:2409:106395:16:0:255:261:403:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:2
48:2410:106396:0:0:264:358:411:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f10:A:[λ(A+ ∆)+λ(A)]′
48:2411:106411:0:9:360:364:420:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:C:(
48:2412:106412:9:0:365:466:411:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:log(A+ ∆)−log(A)
48:2413:106428:0:9:466:470:420:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:C:)
48:2414:106429:9:33:472:475:411:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f9:C:.
48:2415:106430:33:18:89:539:378:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:The idea of averaging weights across two periods is due to T ̈ornqvist (1936).  Propo-
48:2416:106516:18:18:71:539:360:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:sition 10 relates the macroeconomic impact of microeconomic shocks to the size of the
48:2417:106601:18:18:71:460:342:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f5:B:shock and the corresponding Domar weights before and after the shock.43
48:2418:106672:18:18:89:539:324:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:We measure the price of oil using the West Texas Intermediate Spot Crude Oil price
48:2419:106754:18:17:71:539:306:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:from the Federal Reserve Database.  Global crude oil production, measured in thousand
48:2420:106839:17:18:71:539:289:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:tonne of oil equivalents, is from the OECD. World GDP, in current USD, is from the World
48:2421:106927:18:18:71:539:271:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Bank national accounts data. The choice of the pre and post Domar weight is not especially
48:2422:107017:18:21:71:539:253:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:controversial.  Crude oil, as a fraction of world GDP, increased from 1.8% in 1972 to 7.6%
48:2423:107107:21:8:82:482:232:7:g_d0_f5:7:g_d0_f6:F:43One can always compute the full nonlinear impact of a shock on output by computing∫A+∆
48:2424:107195:8:0:465:494:224:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f5:F:Aλ(
48:2425:107198:0:14:498:539:231:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F: ̃A) d log ̃A,
48:2426:107212:14:12:71:539:217:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:and our formula approximates this integral by performing a first-order (log) approximation of the Domar
48:2427:107315:12:12:71:539:205:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:weightλ( ̃A) or equivalently a second-order (translog) approximation of aggregate output.   In theory,  if
48:2428:107421:12:16:71:539:193:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:TFP is a continuous diffusion then one can disaggregate time-periods and compute the impact of shocks
48:2429:107522:16:0:71:203:177:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:over a time period [t,t+δ] as
48:2430:107551:0:14:206:223:187:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f9:F:∫t+δ
48:2431:107555:14:8:210:540:173:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f5:F:tλ(As) d log(As) which can be seen as a repeated application of Hulten’s
48:2432:107627:8:12:71:539:165:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:theorem at every point in time over infinitesimal intervals of time.  However, when TFP has jumps, then
48:2433:107730:12:12:71:539:153:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:this decomposition no longer applies.  In any case, even when it does apply, and when the required high-
48:2434:107834:12:11:71:539:141:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:frequency data regarding TFP shocks and Domar weights is available, it can only be useful ex post to asses
48:2435:107940:11:12:71:540:130:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:the changes in aggregate output over an elapsed period of time due to the TFP shocks d log(As) to a given
48:2436:108045:12:12:71:540:118:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:sector given the observed path of Domar weightλ(As).  It is of no use ex ante to predict how these future
48:2437:108150:12:12:71:539:106:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:shocks will affect aggregate output because one would need to know how the Domar weight will change
48:2438:108249:12:12:71:539:94:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:over time as a result of the shocks, and hence of no use to run counterfactuals.  This latter part is precisely
48:2439:108360:12:0:71:411:82:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:what the second-order approximation at the heart of our paper accomplishes.
49:2440:108437:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:in 1979.  Reassuringly, the Domar weight is back down to its pre-crisis level by 1986 (see
49:2441:108527:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Figure 7). This means that, taking the second-order terms into account, we need to weight
49:2442:108616:18:18:72:539:672:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the shock to the oil industry by 1/2(1.8%+7.6%)=4.7%.  Hence, the second-order terms
49:2443:108700:18:231:72:309:654:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:amplify the shock by a factor of 4.7/1.8≈2.6.
49:2444:108745:231:0:179:427:423:7:g_d0_f5:7:g_d0_f5:F:19701980199020002010
49:2445:108765:0:0:180:185:430:7:g_d0_f5:7:g_d0_f5:F:0
49:2446:108766:0:0:168:184:478:7:g_d0_f5:7:g_d0_f5:F:0.02
49:2447:108770:0:0:168:184:525:7:g_d0_f5:7:g_d0_f5:F:0.04
49:2448:108774:0:0:168:184:573:7:g_d0_f5:7:g_d0_f5:F:0.06
49:2449:108778:0:221:168:184:621:7:g_d0_f5:7:g_d0_f5:F:0.08
49:2450:108782:221:33:121:490:400:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Figure 7: Global expenditures on crude oil as a fraction of world GDP.
49:2451:108852:33:18:89:539:367:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Calibrating the size of the shock to the oil industry is more tricky, since it is not directly
49:2452:108946:18:18:72:540:349:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:observed. If we assume that oil is an endowment, then we can measure the shock simply
49:2453:109031:18:18:72:540:331:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:via changes in the physical quantity of production. To do this, we demean the log growth
49:2454:109119:18:18:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:rate in global crude oil production, and take the shock to be the cumulative change in
49:2455:109205:18:18:72:477:295:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f5:B:demeaned growth rates from 1973 to 1980, which gives us a shock of−13%.44
49:2456:109278:18:33:89:497:277:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Putting this altogether, the first-order impact on aggregate output is therefore
49:2457:109358:33:32:248:363:244:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:1.8%×−13%=−.23%
49:2458:109373:32:28:72:425:212:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:On the other hand, the second-order impact on aggregate output is
49:2459:109438:28:16:220:226:184:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:1
49:2460:109439:16:0:220:226:168:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:2
49:2461:109440:0:24:227:392:176:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f9:A:(1.8%+7.6%)×−13%=−.61%.
49:2462:109463:24:15:82:539:152:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:44We use the demeaned growth rate to remove the overall (positive) trend in production.  Intuitively, if
49:2463:109567:15:12:72:540:137:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:everything is growing at the same rate, then a negative oil shock is a reduction in the growth of oil relative
49:2464:109677:12:12:72:540:125:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:to trend.  Of course, one can easily quibble with this estimate of the size of the shock, but fortunately, the
49:2465:109787:12:12:72:540:113:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:degree of amplification (defined as the ratio of the second-order approximation to the first) is independent
49:2466:109895:12:12:72:540:101:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:of our estimate for the size of the shock.  So, for any value of the shock, the second-order approximation
49:2467:110001:12:0:72:239:89:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:almost triples the impact of the shock.
50:2468:110042:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Hence,  accounting  for  the  second-order  terms  amplifies  the  impact  of  the  oil  shocks
50:2469:110137:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:significantly, so that oil shocks can be macroeconomically significant even without any
50:2470:110224:18:41:72:260:672:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f5:B:financial or demand side frictions.45
50:2471:110261:41:26:72:405:631:14:g_d0_f4:14:g_d0_f4:C:6.3    Baumol’s Cost Disease and Long-Run Growth
50:2472:110309:26:18:72:540:605:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Our final empirical exercise looks to quantify the importance of nonlinearities on long-
50:2473:110397:18:18:72:540:587:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:run productivity growth.  For this exercise, we use World KLEMS data for the US from
50:2474:110481:18:18:72:127:569:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:1948-2014.
50:2475:110491:18:18:89:539:551:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:The “nonlinear” measure of aggregate TFP growth over the sample is built by updating
50:2476:110575:18:41:72:247:533:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the Domar weights every period:
50:2477:110606:41:0:172:268:492:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:C:∆logTFPnonlinear=
50:2478:110623:0:29:276:288:507:8:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f8:C:N∑
50:2479:110625:29:0:274:285:478:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f5:F:i=1
50:2480:110628:0:29:294:310:507:8:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f8:C:2014∑
50:2481:110633:29:0:290:314:478:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f5:F:t=1948
50:2482:110639:0:40:316:439:492:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f9:C:λi,t(logAi,t+1−log(Ai,t)).
50:2483:110665:40:18:71:539:452:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:This provides an approximation, by discrete left Riemann sums, of the exact aggregate
50:2484:110750:18:0:71:355:434:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:TFP growth, given by the sum of continuous integrals
50:2485:110802:0:12:359:375:443:12:g_d0_f8:8:g_d0_f7:C:∑N
50:2486:110804:12:0:369:381:431:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f5:F:i=1
50:2487:110807:0:16:383:407:445:12:g_d0_f8:8:g_d0_f5:C:∫2014
50:2488:110812:16:13:388:470:429:8:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:1948λi,tdlogAi,t.
50:2489:110829:13:18:89:539:416:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Now consider the following counterfactual:  imagine that the economy is loglinear so
50:2490:110913:18:18:72:540:398:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:that the Domar weights are constant throughout the sample at their 1948 values. Assuming
50:2491:111001:18:18:72:540:380:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:that the path for industry-level TFP is unchanged, aggregate TFP growth over the sample
50:2492:111088:18:41:72:171:362:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:would be given by
50:2493:111105:41:0:174:267:321:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:C:∆logTFP1st order=
50:2494:111122:0:30:276:287:337:8:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f8:C:N∑
50:2495:111124:30:0:273:285:307:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f5:F:i=1
50:2496:111127:0:39:289:437:321:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f9:C:λi,1948(logAi,2014−logAi,1948),
50:2497:111158:39:18:89:539:282:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:By  comparing  actual  aggregate  TFP  growth  and  TFP  growth  in  the  counterfactual
50:2498:111246:18:18:72:540:264:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:loglinear economy, we quantify the importance of Baumol’s cost disease (Baumol, 1967):
50:2499:111332:18:18:72:540:246:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the notion that over time, because of complementarities, the shares of low-productivity-
50:2500:111420:18:18:72:540:228:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:growth industries increase while those of high-productivity-growth industries decrease,
50:2501:111507:18:18:72:314:210:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:thereby slowing down aggregate TFP growth.
50:2502:111549:18:19:89:539:192:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:By  the  end  of  the  sample,  aggregate  TFP  growth  in  the  counterfactual  loglinear  or
50:2503:111643:19:16:82:539:173:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:45As noted by Hamilton (2013), first-order approximations of efficient models assign a relatively small
50:2504:111746:16:12:72:540:157:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:impact to oil price shocks. Hence, the literature has tended to focus on various frictions that may account for
50:2505:111857:12:12:72:540:145:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:the strong statistical relationship between oil shocks and aggregate output.  Our calculations suggests that
50:2506:111965:12:12:72:540:133:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:nonlinearities in production, even in an efficient model, may help to explain the outsized effect of oil shocks.
50:2507:112077:12:12:72:540:121:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Furthermore,  our  calculation  also  makes  no  allowance  for  amplification  of  shocks  through  endogenous
50:2508:112188:12:11:72:540:109:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:labor supply and capital accumulation, which are the standard channels for amplification of shocks in the
50:2509:112293:11:12:72:540:98:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:business cycle literature. Hence, coupled with the standard amplification mechanisms of those models, we
50:2510:112397:12:0:72:360:86:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:would expect the reduction in aggregate output to be even larger.
51:2511:112464:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Cobb-Douglas  economy  is  around  87%  whereas  actual  TFP  grew  by  68%.   Hence  the
51:2512:112553:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:presence of nonlinearities slowed down aggregate TFP growth by around 19 percentage
51:2513:112636:18:253:72:233:672:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:points over the sample period.
51:2514:112666:253:0:159:466:419:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:F:194019501960197019801990200020102020
51:2515:112702:0:0:148:166:427:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:F:1.00
51:2516:112706:0:0:148:166:477:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:F:1.20
51:2517:112710:0:0:148:166:527:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:F:1.40
51:2518:112714:0:0:148:166:577:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:F:1.60
51:2519:112718:0:164:148:166:627:9:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f5:F:1.80
51:2520:112722:164:11:400:431:463:6:g_d0_f5:6:g_d0_f5:F:Nonlinear
51:2521:112731:11:10:400:434:452:6:g_d0_f5:6:g_d0_f5:F:FirstOrder
51:2522:112741:10:46:400:443:442:6:g_d0_f5:6:g_d0_f5:F:SecondOrder
51:2523:112752:46:15:72:539:396:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Figure 8:  Cumulative change inTFP:  nonlinear (actual), first-order approximation, and
51:2524:112839:15:31:72:226:381:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:second-order approximation.
51:2525:112866:31:18:89:540:350:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Baumol’s cost disease is a manifestation of nonlinearities, since∆logTFP1st orderis a
51:2526:112951:18:18:72:540:332:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:first-order approximation of actual aggregate TFP growth. A second-order approximation,
51:2527:113038:18:41:72:355:314:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:which captures some of the nonlinearities, is given by
51:2528:113092:41:0:143:240:273:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:C:∆logTFP2nd order=
51:2529:113109:0:17:245:251:281:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:1
51:2530:113110:17:0:245:251:264:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:2
51:2531:113111:0:29:259:271:288:8:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f8:C:N∑
51:2532:113113:29:0:256:268:259:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f5:F:i=1
51:2533:113116:0:40:270:468:273:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f9:A:(λi,1948+λi,2014)(logAi,2014−logAi,1948).
51:2534:113157:40:18:89:539:233:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:In Figure 8, we plot aggregate TFP growth using the nonlinear, first-order, and second-
51:2535:113244:18:18:71:539:215:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:order measures.  The gap between the nonlinear measure and the first-order approxima-
51:2536:113329:18:18:71:539:197:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:tion is sizeable, but that between the nonlinear measure and the second-order approxi-
51:2537:113415:18:18:71:197:179:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:mation is much smaller.
51:2538:113438:18:18:89:539:161:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:These findings are in some sense more general than Baumol’s hypothesized mecha-
51:2539:113517:18:18:71:539:143:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:nism. Traditionally, the mechanism for Baumol’s cost disease is through prices: because of
51:2540:113607:18:18:71:539:125:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:complementarities, as the prices of high-productivity-growth industries fall, their shares
51:2541:113697:18:18:71:539:107:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:in aggregate output fall, reducing aggregate TFP growth. Alternative stories for structural
51:2542:113788:18:17:71:539:89:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:transformation emphasize other mechanisms, principally, non-homotheticities whereby
51:2543:113871:17:0:71:539:72:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:as real income increases, consumers increase their expenditures on industries that happen
52:2544:113962:0:18:72:539:708:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:to  have  lower  productivity  growth.46From  the  perspective  of  our  framework,  non-
52:2545:114051:18:18:71:539:690:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:homotheticities and complementarities are both nonlinearities.   Our calculations of the
52:2546:114139:18:46:71:421:672:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f5:B:impact of nonlinearities encompass both types of mechanisms.47,48
52:2547:114204:46:31:72:188:626:17:g_d0_f4:17:g_d0_f4:C:7    Conclusion
52:2548:114219:31:18:72:540:595:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:The paper points to many unanswered questions.  For instance, it shows that the macro
52:2549:114304:18:18:72:540:577:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:impact of a micro shock depends greatly on how quickly factors can be reallocated across
52:2550:114392:18:18:72:540:559:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:production  units.   Since  our  structural  model  is  static,  we  are  forced  to  proxy  for  the
52:2551:114493:18:18:72:540:541:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:temporal  dimension  of  reallocation  by  resorting  to  successive  comparative  statics.   In
52:2552:114589:18:18:72:540:523:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:ongoing work, we investigate the dynamic adjustment process more rigorously and find
52:2553:114673:18:18:72:540:505:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:that  although  we  can  think  of  the  no-reallocation  and  perfect-reallocation  cases  as  the
52:2554:114772:18:18:72:540:487:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:beginning and end of the adjustment, the speed of adjustment also greatly depends on
52:2555:114856:18:18:72:540:469:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the microeconomic details. This means that the dynamic response of aggregate output to
52:2556:114942:18:17:72:539:451:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:different shocks is greatly affected by issues like geographic or sectoral mobility of labor,
52:2557:115035:17:18:72:540:434:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:even  with  perfect  and  complete  markets  that  allow  us  to  abstract  from  distributional
52:2558:115131:18:18:72:540:416:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:issues.   Our model also lacks capital accumulation and endogenous labor supply,  and
52:2559:115216:18:18:72:505:398:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:incorporating these into the present analysis is an interesting area for future work.
52:2560:115301:18:18:89:540:380:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Furthermore,  in  this  paper,  we  have  focused  exclusively  on  the  way  shocks  affect
52:2561:115393:18:18:72:540:362:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:aggregate output. In ongoing work, we take up the related question of how shocks affect
52:2562:115480:18:18:72:540:344:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:non-aggregate outcomes, namely how shocks propagate from one producer to another,
52:2563:115561:18:18:72:540:326:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:and how microeconomic variables comove with one another in a production network.
52:2564:115641:18:18:72:540:308:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Since thei jth element of the Hessian, that we characterize, is also the response of the sales
52:2565:115735:18:18:72:539:290:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:share ofito a shock toj, our formulas are also relevant to understanding comovement in
52:2566:115821:18:18:72:222:272:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:general equilibrium models.
52:2567:115848:18:18:89:539:254:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Finally,  our results ignore non-technological frictions,  but the forces we identify do
52:2568:115936:18:18:72:539:236:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:not  disappear  in  richer  models  with  inefficient  equilibria.   Non-unitary  elasticities  of
52:2569:116034:18:17:72:540:218:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:substitution in networks can amplify or attenuate the underlying frictions. In a companion
52:2570:116124:17:18:72:540:201:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:paper, Baqaee and Farhi (2017), we undertake a systematic characterization of these effects.
52:2571:116216:18:12:82:276:183:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:46See Herrendorf et al. (2013) as an example.
52:2572:116261:12:16:82:539:171:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:47Our theoretical characterizations cover all these nonlinearities.  In fact, formally, non-homotheticities
52:2573:116368:16:12:72:540:155:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:can always be represented via non-unitary elasticities of substitution between inputs and a fixed factor.
52:2574:116473:12:9:72:539:143:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:To see this, note that any non-homothetic functionf(x) can be extended into a constant-returns function
52:2575:116576:9:15:75:539:134:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F: ̃f(x,y)=y f(x/y) wheref(x)= ̃f(x,1). Then, non-homotheticity infis equivalent to a non-unitary elasticity
52:2576:116682:15:8:72:253:119:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:of substitution betweenxandyin ̃f(x,y).
52:2577:116721:8:16:82:539:111:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:48Another potential source of nonlinearities is shocks to the composition of demand. For example, a Cobb-
52:2578:116826:16:12:72:540:95:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Douglas model with shocks to the share parameters is a nonlinear model since the cross-partial derivatives
52:2579:116932:12:11:72:540:83:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:of aggregate output with respect to industry TFP and share shocks are non-zero. Our results also cover such
52:2580:117039:11:0:72:121:72:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:economies.
53:2581:117051:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:We show that in inefficient models, the “second-order” terms that we characterize in this
53:2582:117140:18:47:72:225:690:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:paper can become first order.

63:3675:132517:30:18:72:539:185:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:For the example is Section 3.2,  the economy with extreme complementarityθ=0 has
63:3676:132597:18:18:71:540:167:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f5:B:Y=A/a, where 1/ais the sales to output ratio in steady-state. Therefore, in this example,
63:3677:132686:18:18:71:539:149:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:although Hulten’s approximation fails in log terms, Hulten’s theorem is globally accurate
63:3678:132775:18:18:71:540:131:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:inlinearterms.  In other words, our examples so far may suggest that extreme comple-
63:3679:132859:18:18:71:540:113:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:mentarities can only have outsized effects, in linear terms, if we restrict the movement of
63:3680:132950:18:0:71:193:95:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:labor across industries.
64:3681:132976:0:18:89:539:708:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:However, this impression is false. To see this, consider a slightly more complex exam-
64:3682:133062:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:ple where we generalize the example above by allowing multiple industries.  Aggregate
64:3683:133147:18:18:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:consumption is Cobb-Douglas across goods with equal weights (bi=1/N).  Each good is
64:3684:133230:18:18:72:540:654:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:produced using labor and the good itself as an intermediate input.  We assume full labor
64:3685:133318:18:33:72:317:636:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:reallocation/constant returns to scale. We have
64:3686:133365:33:0:274:293:603:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f6:C:Y=

64:3740:133628:31:18:89:539:304:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:In Figure 9 we plot output as a function of TFP shocks in linear terms.  As promised,
64:3741:133713:18:18:71:539:286:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:this economy features strong aggregate complementarities in the sense that a negative TFP
64:3742:133802:18:18:71:539:268:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:shock can cause a drastic reduction in output even in linear terms, despite the fact that
64:3743:133891:18:18:71:539:250:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:labor can be costlessly reallocated across sectors. This happens because, in equilibrium, a
64:3744:133982:18:18:71:539:232:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:negative shock to industryidoes not result in more labor being allocated to production
64:3745:134068:18:18:71:540:214:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:in industryi. This follows from the fact that consumption has a Cobb-Douglas form, and
64:3746:134154:18:18:71:540:196:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:so the income and substitution effects from a shock toioffset each other.  Since no new
64:3747:134241:18:18:72:540:178:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:labor is allocated toi, ififaces a low structural elasticity of substitutionθi≈0, its output
64:3748:134333:18:17:72:539:160:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:falls dramatically in response to a negative shock.  This can then have a large effect on
64:3749:134422:17:18:72:540:143:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:aggregate consumption. Of course, Cobb-Douglas consumption is simply a clean way to
64:3750:134505:18:18:72:540:125:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:illustrate this intuition.  If the structural elasticity of substitution in consumption where
64:3751:134598:18:0:72:466:107:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:less than unity (θ0<1), then these effects would be even further amplified.

73:4340:146517:47:27:71:539:208:17:g_d0_f4:17:g_d0_f4:C:E    Gains  from  Trade  in  Open  Economies  and  Relation  to
73:4341:146580:27:31:99:137:181:17:g_d0_f4:17:g_d0_f4:C:ACR
73:4342:146583:31:18:71:540:150:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Arkolakis,  Costinot,  and  Rodr ́ıguez-Clare  (2012),  henceforth  ACR,  consider  an  open-
73:4343:146676:18:18:71:539:132:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:economy model with no intermediate inputs and a single factor of production per country.
73:4344:146764:18:18:71:539:114:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:They impose some macro-level restrictions, and prove a powerful characterization of the
73:4345:146851:18:18:71:539:96:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:gains from trade. Namely, they assume that (1) trade is balanced, (2) profits are a constant
73:4346:146943:18:0:71:539:78:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:share of revenues, and (3) import demand system is CES. Using these assumptions, they
74:4347:147030:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:show that the gains from trade, as measured by the change in real income associated with
74:4348:147118:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:going to autarky, is given by the reciprocal of the domestic expenditure share raised to the
74:4349:147210:18:18:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:reciprocal of the trade elasticity.  The ACR result, and its generalizations (summarized in
74:4350:147301:18:18:72:540:654:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Costinot and Rodriguez-Clare, 2014), suggest that one can quantify the gains from trade
74:4351:147388:18:18:72:407:636:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:without needing to directly estimate the size of the trade shock.
74:4352:147453:18:18:89:539:618:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:We now show how under certain conditions, changes in iceberg trade costs in an open-
74:4353:147537:18:18:72:540:600:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:economy model can be recast as productivity shocks in an associated closed-economy
74:4354:147619:18:18:72:540:582:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model.  This then allows us to use our results to study the second-order effects of trade
74:4355:147708:18:18:72:540:564:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:shocks.   For simplicity,  we work with a one-factor model (like ACR), but these results
74:4356:147796:18:18:72:540:546:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:can be extended to the case of multiple factors.  We also restrict ourselves to nested-CES
74:4357:147886:18:18:72:223:528:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:economies in standard form.
74:4358:147913:18:18:89:539:510:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:We start by associating a fictitious nested-CES domestic closed-economy model to the
74:4359:147997:18:18:72:540:492:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:true nested-CES open-economy model, both in standard form.  The closed economy has
